###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Climate models suck - It's the programming

_(reply depth: 39)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2010-03-02T20:45:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<YWkjn.215963$OX4.32712@newsfe25.iad>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<hmk72n$pcr$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<mvydnYwrWIdeQc7WnZ2dnUVZ_jGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <obednW0lbctcbRbWnZ2dnUVZ_gSdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <hmhgi5$p1t$1@reader1.panix.com> <o4iqo5pucpjrb5lk47pmms8vfue14lirqa@4ax.com> <hmk72n$pcr$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <o4iqo5pucpjrb5lk47pmms8vfue14lirqa@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> considered by the Founders in the late 18th.

"To make the punishment fit the crime.....?", although following is 
contradictory :-

http://www.lyricoperasandiego.org/resource_library/MikadoNotes.htm

"Some claim the phrase to "make the punishment fit the crime" seems to 
be original with Gilbert, but like many of his other phrases, it dates 
at least as far back as the Roman orator Cicero in his De Legibus."

Or were you perhaps referencing, (either the Mikado or Pooh Bah), "I am 
  so proud if I allowed .....?".

I nearly replied to your earlier reminisce, "You've got to be taught 
(prejudice).....". Lorenz Hart (originally, back in 30s' 40s') with 
Richard Rodgers and then subsequently Oscar Hammerstein. My favourites 
were Pal Joey, Carousel and The King and I. (The latter - a lovely play 
on words when Charles got engaged to Dianna). Not the TV commentator but 
it came from his cameraman, he referred to "The King and Di".

'South Pacific', I see it's credited with the most favourite numbers. 
Me, last night in UK, Dec 12, 1951, before emplaning for 2.5 boring 
years in Egypt. Teamed up with somebody else also dressed in RAF blue 
and we went, I think, to Drury Lane and saw Mary Martin as Nellie 
Forbush. The most gorgeous American girl played the delicate Bali Hai 
heroine. Stopped backstage to get Mary's autograph. She was no chicken 
even then and real-life Mom of JR (Larry Hagman). She was interested in 
the guys in blue and asked where or what we were doing/going. I replied 
"Suez", approx  English pronunciation 'Soo-is'. She was puzzled and then 
the penny dropped, when I added the word 'Canal'. "Oh !", she said, 
"SOO-EZZ !".

Mitzi Gaynor did the movie version with "I'm gonna wash that man right 
out of my hair...." That's what Mary M. did for EVERY PERFORMANCE night 
or matinee - and she had a short bubble-cut to cope with the constant 
hair washing.

Nearly missed him. There was a young Scot, (some 11 moths older than 
me), a boy sailor who was discharged from the R. Navy on medical grounds 
- stomach problems and I think he still suffers from them. I can't 
guarantee, but he may have been in the chorus line that I saw, "There is 
Nothing Like a Dame....". He subsequently played Richard the Lion Heart 
with an Edinburgh accent, Captain Raimus, again with an Edinburgh accent 
in 'The Hunt for Red October'. No one less than the first 007.

To relieve boredom in Egypt got involved with the 'Theatah' crowd. 
Young, I dovetailed into some roles, as romantic male lead. Tough, I was 
never a Romeo or Casanova. Did have a part opposite a lively Australian 
girl (in the WRAF), Phys Ed instructor(ess), lively, not at all bad 
looking and built like an Amazon warrior :-). That necessitated an 
on-stage kiss. Ouch, embarrassing, but I persisted; it was quite 
enjoyable. Had a five-minute romance with her. Was intending to write 'a 
one night stand' - but that conveys a very  different meaning :-).

We did 'The Man Who Came to Dinner', and I landed the role of a spoof on 
Noel Coward. Monty Woolly was the 'Man' in the stage version and movie 
and a close friend of composer Cole Porter dating back to their days at 
Yale. Porter composed a complicated 'porteresque' piece for the Noel 
Coward to sing. No chance; much too complicated for the WRAF sergeant to 
play the piano off stage. At my suggestion we went with "Shall We Dance 
?" from the King and I. Believe me, a bit challenging to warble the only 
song even in a comedy.

Had some fun with others like "Arsenic and Old Lace". I did the Raymond 
Massey bit, front of suit stuffed with cushions to give me sinister 
bulk, plus a scar painted on face.

Coming towards the end of my tour,(Dec '53 - home in June/July '54), 
somebody suggested a Variety Show for Christmas. Quick skits, songs etc. 
Contemplate, we made a grab for the only movie house on the camp to do 
our theatrical stuff, so wasn't always exactly received with enthusiasm 
for those who wanted their movies - which were all up-to-date releases 
in tune with London releases.

Yeah. I got involved in quick skits, and we did a chorus of 'There is 
Nothing Like a Dame.....'. But I thought to myself, 'What the hell ?'. 
Somebody dreamed up this club scene. Yes a Jewish kid did an 
impersonation of Danny Kaye - who else ? A buxom Scottish lassie did a 
nice number, 'The Roses of Picardy'. I asked, "Can I join in ?". Being 
well in the mix no objection. "What can you do ?". They liked the 
singing demo. So as part of the cabaret this sailor comes on stage. 
Dress rehearsal I looked like somebody from Hong Kong. Back to the 
office and with scissors and cardboard cut myself a RN sailor's hat 
complete with a hat band. Now it looked a bit more real.

Comes the night of the show. Only game in town and the movie theatre was 
full, front rows, the officers in their short dinner jackets and 
be-medaled, and behind them the lads, particularly the RAF Regiment 
Wing. Why I don't know, they were nicknamed 'rock-apes'. (RAF infantry 
to defend airfields in the event of ground attack). Sure some were 
rock-apes, and they wouldn't be impressed with anything that bored them 
to death.

Scotch lassie comes on and renders her song. Polite applause. Then yours 
truly having, Judy Garland style, sunk a half-bottle of three-star 
Martell before hitting the stage. I was accompanied by an impromptu 
four-piece band. Curiosity, 'What's this one about ?'. I started off in 
slow motion :

My doll is as dainty as a sparrow
Her figure is something to applaud
Where she's narrow
She's as narrow as an arrow
And she's broad
Where a B-R-O-A-D should be bro-ooo-aud Ha !

A Hundred and one
pounds of fun
That's my Little HoneyBun
Get a load of Honey Bun to-n-n-night.....

I did it only once and then walked off-stage. The bloody place erupted; 
if not yet rock-apes, they soon became rock-apes ! Others in the theatre 
group liked my thing from dress rehearsals, (even suggesting hand 
movements which gave the song an additional bash), but we were all 
astounded at the reception. No wonder show biz personalities get duped 
into a false allusion when receiving adulation from their audiences.

Great fun - but I have to say, 'Pal Joey' (with Hart) and the others 
with Hammerstein 'Carousel' and "The King and I', are my favourites.

Never done the theatha bit since - but enjoy the memories. Should've had 
a crack at acting when I left RAF; '61 and just about the time Sean was 
selected to play 007 - selected by Producer Chubby (?) Broccoli's wife. 
She had the hots for the young actor up on the test screening.

Still as Micheal Caine said on that useless, Regis Philbean show : "Well 
back then they didn't send your pogie (unemployment money) through the 
mail. You had to stand in-line to collect it. And my mate Sean Connery 
was two places in front of me". They just happened to be two lucky 
members of the theatrical crowd that made it.

Jimmy
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Climate models suck - It's the programming

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

```
In article <YWkjn.215963$OX4.32712@newsfe25.iad>,
James J. Gavan <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

[snip]

>> If everyone is among 'the guilty' then the logical conclusion would be to 
>> just drag someone out of the crowd, Mr Brazee... that would be a 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>  so proud if I allowed .....?".

The answer is D) None of the Above, Mr Gavan... see 
<http://books.google.com/books?id=ZgVUqbK-_1EC&lpg=PP1&dq=gilbert%20sullivan%20mikado&pg=PA8#v=onepage&q=&f=false> 
and page down to the bottom of Page 8 and then on to Page 9; you may see 
something like:

--begin quoted text:

SONG--KO-KO with CHORUS OF MEN

As some day it may happen that a victim must be found,
 I've got a little list--I've got a little list
Of society offenders who might well be underground,
 And who never would be missed--who never would be missed!
There's the pestilential nuisances who write for autographs--
All the people who have flabby hands and irritating laughs--
All the children who are up in dates , and floor you with 'em flat--
All the people who shake hands with you, shake hands with you like that--
All the third persons who on spoiling tete-a-tetes insist--
 They'd none of 'em be missed--they'd none of 'em be missed!

CHORUS. He's got 'em on the list--he's got 'em on the list;
         And they'll none of 'em be missed--they'll none of 'em be
             missed.

--end quoted text

The goal is not to find one who committed a crime, just that 'a victim 
must be found'.  May all who read this (and those who don't, as well) be 
blessed and kept from such a model of justice.

>
>I nearly replied to your earlier reminisce, "You've got to be taught 
>(prejudice).....".

[snip of a wonderful reminisce]

A good thing that you did now, Mr Gavan... I recall flipping through 
channel-after-channel of worthless garbage on an insomniac night and my 
thumb froze at the sight of a black-and-white clip of a woman smiling and 
singing 'Come and meet... those dancing feee-eeee-eeeeet'... dear Lord, it 
was Ruby Keeler and the 'long number' from '42nd Street'... and Dick 
Powell was still there, too... and they were so young, so young.

DD

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Climate models suck - It's the programming

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-02-08T13:32:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7t94ddFhujU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<mvydnYwrWIdeQc7WnZ2dnUVZ_jGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <4KOdnbXYY6vHw_7WnZ2dnUVZ_oSdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <hk2ts2$pjc$2@reader1.panix.com> <UKadnc3GWuTvRPXWnZ2dnUVZ_iydnZ2d@earthlink.com> <hkc5lg$1mc$1@reader2.panix.com> <DN-dnWRymJYjyPHWnZ2dnUVZ_sCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7t3kt1Fg5kU1@mid.individual.net> <dbstm59c1rkmasuq6kf3qqtis7hqg6gho3@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sat, 6 Feb 2010 11:37:18 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> do about it".

OK, last post. (a bugle sounds in the distance... :-))

1. People who proudly proclaim responsibility for crimes against civilians 
that most of us couldn't even imagine, do not need to "defend their 
innocence", and there is no need for us to provide a process whereby they 
can. They are NOT innocent and they DON'T WANT to be seen as innocent, so 
why waste time and resources?

We MUST (and do) provide due process for people who are not in this 
category.

2. I believe there is a level of action that is way above a "felony" (just 
as a "felony" supersedes a "misdemeanour" and the justice system recognizes 
this in most civilized countries.). I would like to see the justice system 
updated to deal with such "supercrime", and part of that update would be 
that people guilty of it do not receive the rights that people NOT guilty of 
it do. There would be one penalty; humane execution.

I realise these are very radical ideas but I believe they are worth 
exploring and have attempted to do so by interaction in this forum.

I can see that most people see it as an attack on Human Rights (which, 
ironically, I am a very staunch defender of...) and some very valid issues 
have been raised about the pitfalls that could be found in such a system. 
(Like, how do you decide what is "beyond the Pale"?)

As I have nothing more to contribute, and have gathered enough from the 
responses to persuade me that radical reform will be fiercely resisted, 
there is little point in me posting further on this topic.

Nevertheless, it is interesting to me that the United States has actually 
taken some moves down this path (see HeyBub's posts).

The question that has to be asked is: "Should Homo Sapiens who demonstrate 
by their actions that they do NOT have some of the attributes which we 
consider to be "human", be treated as humans and afforded the same rights 
that the rest of us enjoy?"

Whether your answer is "they should" or "they shouldn't" it is well worth 
looking at the issue.

No more from me on this.

Pete.
.
```

>> You say it isn't about the accused getting a right to defend their
>> innocence.     So what exactly, *is* this discussion about?    What is
>> it that you want changed?

>OK, last post. (a bugle sounds in the distance... :-))

Sounds good.

>1. People who proudly proclaim responsibility for crimes against civilians 
>that most of us couldn't even imagine, do not need to "defend their 
>innocence", and there is no need for us to provide a process whereby they 
>can. They are NOT innocent and they DON'T WANT to be seen as innocent, so 
>why waste time and resources?

Due process allows for someone to make a guilty plea.    Just make
sure it isn't coerced.    Apparently a significant number of people in
Gitmo deny guilt when not being tortured.

>We MUST (and do) provide due process for people who are not in this 
>category.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>it do. There would be one penalty; humane execution.

Lots of people don't like execution because it is difficult to undo
when someone is exonerated.   But it is just as difficult to give back
someone's prison time.    And for my tastes, "humane execution" is too
humane - these guys deserve worse - life in prison.    But I will
accept that you don't think the guilty need to be punished as much as
I do.    Or you disagree with the principles Nathan Hale and think
humane execution *is* worse than life in prison.   Some people do, I
am nowhere close to that belief.

The main problem (for me) with execution is when it is used to hide
mistakes.    I hope that my country wouldn't use it that way, but it
has been used that way around the world quite a bit.


>I realise these are very radical ideas but I believe they are worth 
>exploring and have attempted to do so by interaction in this forum.

Again, I missed these "very radical ideas".   You are in favor of
capital punishment for convicted terrorist.   That's not radical.

You may be in favor of accepting a coerced confession and skipping the
trial.   That certainly would be very radical, if common among
despotic states.    If so, that's one idea, not multiple.

>I can see that most people see it as an attack on Human Rights (which, 
>ironically, I am a very staunch defender of...) and some very valid issues 
>have been raised about the pitfalls that could be found in such a system. 
>(Like, how do you decide what is "beyond the Pale"?)

We don't need to define "beyond the Pale".    Just define which crimes
have which punishments.    Often this tends to be a matter of fashion.
For instance, it appears that prison sentences for murder are much
smaller than prison sentences for having child porn at the current
time.     The punishment for someone who explodes a truck bomb in
Oklahoma city for political purposes could be different from someone
who puts a bomb in an airplane over Colorado to get his mother's life
insurance.

>As I have nothing more to contribute, and have gathered enough from the 
>responses to persuade me that radical reform will be fiercely resisted, 
>there is little point in me posting further on this topic.

The only thing radical you implied was not actually stated.   (which I
discussed above).     Or maybe you didn't imply it, and I only
inferred it.

>Nevertheless, it is interesting to me that the United States has actually 
>taken some moves down this path (see HeyBub's posts).
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>No more from me on this.

That's your right.    I wish though that you had stated what radical
reform you wanted made.    The one I inferred above has significant
pitfalls, and I could not find any other specific reform mentioned.

```
SkippyPB wrote:
>
> "Enemy Combatants"....a phrase created to ignore the constitution.
> If we go to war to defend our Constitution then those we go to war
> against should be held accountable to it and under it, not be ignored
> by it.

No, "enemy combatants" is a phrase coined by the 4th Geneva Convention. An 
enemy combatant is one possessing the four characteristics I mentioned 
earlier. This Convention went into some detail regarding exceptions (such as 
a hurriedly-organized militia to defend against an invasion), and folks who 
opperate on the periphery of combat such as fortification builders, truck 
drivers carryng war material, medical and social workers, other government 
workers (i.e. postmen) who have a minimal impact on the conflict, and dozens 
of other permutations.

Also note that the Constitution and TREATIES MADE PURSUANT THERETO are the 
supreme law of the land. The U.S. ratified all the Geneva treaties and Hauge 
protocols. These treaties have the same force as our Constitution.

The phrase "unlawful enemy combatant" was coined by our Supreme Court when 
it was asked to deal with German saboteurs who were put ashore on Long 
Island (Ex parte Quirin 317 U.S. 1 - 1942). There the court held:

"By universal agreement and practice, the law of war draws a distinction 
between the armed forces and the peaceful populations of belligerent nations 
and also between those who are lawful and unlawful combatants. Lawful 
combatants are subject to capture and detention as prisoners of war by 
opposing military forces. Unlawful combatants are likewise subject to 
capture and detention, but in addition they are subject to trial and 
punishment by military tribunals for acts which render their belligerency 
unlawful. The spy who secretly and without uniform passes the military lines 
of a belligerent in time of war, seeking to gather military information and 
communicate it to the enemy, or an enemy combatant who without uniform comes 
secretly through the lines for the purpose of waging war by destruction of 
life or property, are familiar examples of belligerents who are generally 
deemed not to be entitled to the status of prisoners of war, but to be 
offenders against the law of war subject to trial and punishment by military 
tribunals.

"By a long course of practical administrative construction by its military 
authorities, our Government has recognized that those who during time of war 
pass surreptitiously from enemy territory into our own, discarding their 
uniforms upon entry, for the commission of hostile acts involving 
destruction of life or property, have the status of unlawful combatants 
punishable as such by military commission."

(In my view, this last paragraph exactly describes the Christmas bomber.)

The Supreme Court went on to say:

"We conclude that the Fifth and Sixth Amendments did not restrict whatever 
authority was conferred by the Constitution to try offenses against the law 
of war by military commission, and that petitioners, charged with such an 
offense not required to be tried by jury at common law, were lawfully placed 
on trial by the Commission without a jury."

Quirin and his buddies were hanged.
```

On Wed, 3 Feb 2010 13:58:48 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>I don't think they were "wrong", but I DO think the Constitution needs an 
>update in the light of what transpired on 9/11.

While I don't agree that 9/11 is what should give the update, I do
believe that we need to define the legal status of what has replaced
declared war.     This is a problem older than 9/11, but would address
your concerns.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-02-03T14:40:06+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7ss2foFq7gU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7rtoeoFd3qU1@mid.individual.net> <m4udnYvpBYJU_v7WnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <b8p8m5lupl8mh3a3n397vu1nl02cqofb2s@4ax.com> <7ZCdnT8njLIj-PXWnZ2dnUVZ_oWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <hka1pg$79e$2@reader2.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <7ZCdnT8njLIj-PXWnZ2dnUVZ_oWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>,
> HeyBub <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> this particularly difficult to apply.

Nothing in the language... (the language enables us to examine these 
arguments and have this discussion).

The question is whether every member of the species "Homo Sapiens" is a 
human/person.

At one level, we would like to think so and will strive for freedom and 
justice and equal opportunity for ALL. Then a member of this species goes 
and does something that is SO "beyond the pale" it really makes us question 
exactly what it means to be "human".

Is being human just belonging to a biological phylum or species?

Many people would have thought so until 9/11.

Now it has to be questioned.

Terrorists and Terrorism behave in ways which can only be described as 
"inhuman". Merciless, cold-blooded, prepared to do things that a "normal" 
human being would not even conceive of.

We have seen terrible acts across history, but even Atilla the Hun, Ghenghiz 
Khan, and Adolf Hitler would probably not have done what was done on 9/11, 
even if they HAD that capability. They committed brutal and savage acts of 
war, and sometimes attempts at genocide (probably stemming from derangement 
due to power), but they didn't necessarily simply attack unarmed civilians 
with whom there was no formal quarrel. When Hitler invaded the Sudetenland, 
he didn't just roll over the villages and villagers, Ghenghiz Khan traded 
with foreign nations once he was satisfied they were no threat, Atilla 
become a much loved leader in his Empire because his rule brought stability 
in which trade could prosper.

Modern Terrorists are not in the same league as these examples of 
"monsters".

Despite some really bad behaviour by various people throughout history, 
there is no precedent for what we are now facing.

Modern technology has put unthinkable horror into the hands of "people" who 
are perfectly prepared to use it in the attainment of their own ends. They 
have already demonstrated no qualms about what they do and are prepared to 
bring down, not just Western Society, but conceivably the whole of 
civilization.

They have no limits imposed by decency, morality, or what is generally 
referred to as "humanity".

It is therefore a fair question to ask if they should be considered as 
"human" simply on the basis of their biological origin.

In my opinion, it can no longer be taken as read that the "No person" in 
Amendment V should be applied to everybody.

"No person ...UNLESS said person has indicated by their actions against 
property and people in our society that they should not be subject to the 
rights and priveleges this charter confers and must be dealt with by the 
special provisions for Terrorism and Terrorist acts covered in Section..."

The Section would explore the subtle differences between standard criminal 
behaviour carried out by people against each other, and the specific nature 
of what constitutes a "crime against humanity" for which all bets are off.

We have a different code for menial offences and serious ones (misdemeanours 
and felonies... most countries I have lieved in implement these concepts, 
even if they don't do it exactly the way the USA does...), what is so hard 
about having a SUPER offence category with special means for dealing with 
it?


Pete.
```

Bingo.  Thank you, Mr Dashwood, for not forcing me to raise this thorny 
issue.  Laws, rights, processes... these are nothing unless one first 
decides what is 'a human' to whom they are to be applied.

The Founders did *not* believe that every creature of human form, capable 
of interbreeding with others of human form, were 'humans'... hence that 
bit in Article I, Section 2 of '... the whole number of free persons, 
including those bound to service for a term of years, and excluding 
Indians not taxed, three fifths of all other Persons.'

'Bound to service'... a brain-damaged indentured servant was to be counted 
in full, for terms of representation and taxation, while the equivalent of 
George Washington Carver - unless he could produce a writ of manumission - 
was 40% less.  'A necessary compromise', some whine, while others shudder 
and remember the Bell in Philadelphia (ordered in 1851 and cast by the 
Whitechapel Bell Foundry) bearing the words 'And ye shall hallow the 
fiftieth year, and proclaim liberty throughout all the land unto all the 
inhabitants thereof; it shall be a jubile unto you; and ye shall return 
every man unto his possessions, and ye shall return every man unto his 
family'... and the Civil War that had to be fought to bring these words 
closer to the Way Things Are.

[snip]

>human being would not even conceive of.

cow when Everyone Knows bovines contain ancestral spirits?

[snip]

>Despite some really bad behaviour by various people throughout history, 
>there is no precedent for what we are now facing.

All the more reason to act quickly and with as little thought as 
possible... and I am the King of England.

[snip]

>referred to as "humanity".

adherance'... that way lies the Inquisition.

Alistair wrote:
> On Jan 29, 6:15 pm, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
>> On Thu, 28 Jan 2010 15:52:45 -0600, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com>
…[64 more quoted lines elided]…
> or UNLAWFUL ENEMY COMBATANTS appears in the Geneva convention.

Sure. And the definition of "lawful combatant" DOES appear in the 3th Geneva 
Convention, Article 2(B) as one who wears a uniform, has a chain of command, 
carries arms openly, and follows the rules of war. By extension, a 
belligerent not meeting the requirements of "lawful combatant" is an 
"unlawful" enemy combatant.

Yes. The conventions and protocols cover those aiding the war effort but not 
in the carrying of arms: aid workers, construction workers building 
fortifications, civilian truck drivers carrying war material, workers in 
armaments plants, and so on. Also defined are those civilians who are 
hastily organized into a defense militia to repel and invasion. The 
protocols even go so far as to describe non-belligerent government workers 
such as police and firemen, letter carriers, etc.

From the German's perspective, members of the French Resistance were 
unlawful enemy combatants.

SkippyPB wrote:
> On Thu, 28 Jan 2010 15:52:45 -0600, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
> wrote:
…[56 more quoted lines elided]…
> by it.

I found that interesting, Steve.

I can't see your reasoning.

"If we go to war to defend _OUR_ Constitution..."  (emphasis is mine)

Why would an enemy need for even a heartbeat to be included in something 
which they are going to war against?

Suppose, just for the sake of exploring this a bit further, it weren't the 
Constitution we were going to war to defend. Suppose it was the right to 
wear loud shirts in public.

Presumably, the "enemy" feel equally strongly about NOT wearing loud shirts 
in public.

So, does this mean that every captured enemy soldier shall be made to wear a 
loud shirt? So they can be included in _OUR_ system?

Seems a bit inhumane and arbitrary, doesn't it?

I may be wrong about this, but it seems fair and reasonable to me that 
people who are so vehemently opposed to a certain system that they will wage 
war against it, cannot reasonably be included in it, and they probably don't 
want to be either.

I think we should respect their right to NOT be included in the 
Constitutional rights OUR Citizens enjoy (and which they have shown by their 
actions they despise and scorn) and if we manage to catch the people who fly 
airplanes into buildings or leave bombs in school buses, or underground 
railways, we should simply treat them as what they are: "The Enemy".

A little needle in the back of the hand (a firing squad equates them with 
soldiers, and that is an insult to anyone who has ever been a soldier...) 
would save the courts, taxpayers, and bleeding hearts a heap of time and 
money, and let us get on with the task of finding (and needling) the rest of 
them...

Guess it's just as well I don't rule the world... :-)

Pete.
```

SkippyPB wrote:
> On Sun, 31 Jan 2010 02:40:10 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[108 more quoted lines elided]…
> who are waging a war against us?

It depends on HOW they are waging the war against us. I think HeyBub made 
the point that legal combatants are entitled to rights and protection; 
illegal ones are not. Think about the images of those people jumping from 
the buring skyscrapers. Does that seem like a "legal" or fair war to you? 
Suppose it was YOUR family? And it isn't about getting revenge. It is about 
treating people who are capable of such acts the way they deserve to be 
treated.

I guess my point is that people who commit crimes against humanity should 
not then enjoy the same rights we afford to our own citizens, even when they 
commit criminal acts.

I would argue that giving them such is what demeans our society. Why would 
anyone bother to be a citizen (law-abiding or otherwise) when some murdering 
fanatic gets exactly the same rights as you do? Doesn't that devalue the 
rights of normal people?

As you probably know, I will defend to the death the rights of people to be 
heard and to have their say; but that stops when they start murdering a 
civilian population without pause or remorse.

I see people who behave like that as a cancer in the world. There is no 
current cure for cancer other than to cut it out...


>If we ignore or suspend those
> guarantees, then we put ourselves at their level.

No, I beg to differ. If we ignore or suspend guaranteed rights for people 
who are ENTITLED to those rights  then we are definitely out of order.

The key to this argument is whether people who have committed certain 
hostile acts against the community, in a manner that is outside acceptable 
forms of dissent, and leading to a threat to the safety and security of the 
citizens in the community, are still entitled to the same rights and 
safeguards that members of the community enjoy.

I don't think they do.

It's a bit like the IRA campaign in England. Only when they realized that 
every atrocity was further alienating the public away from their cause, that 
their Terrorism was not working, and they were increasingly becoming pariahs 
outside of a civilised society, did they start to think about a political 
solution. For Bin Laden it has gone too far and he must answer for what he 
has done. But that doesn't mean there could NEVER be discussion or dialogue 
with groups like Al Queda... If Bin Laden is removed or brought to justice, 
his replacement could certainly forswear violence for a period while 
dialogue takes place. My point it that it isn't insoluble as long as you 
don't get to crashing planes into buildings and similar acts that put you 
outside the realm of "rights".

>We have ideals and
> morals that are expounded on in the US Constitution.

And they have been an inspiration and a light to many peoples who wish to 
abide by the rule of law.

My issue here is not with the US Constitution, it is with the people who 
seek to undermine it by the foulest possible means.


>If we (US
> citizens) are not going to abide by them 100% of the time, then what's
> the point?

You MUST abide by them 100% of the time for people who are entitled to those 
rights. At the time the Constitution was written it was a different world. 
There wasn't the capability to commit crimes against humanity on the scale 
we have seen since. Had there been, it is likely that the architects of the 
Constitution, in their wisdom, would have noted that it was possible for 
people, by their actions, to lose the rights conferred by the Constitution.

I understand what you're saying, but I don't agree.

People who wage a cowardly and despicable war against a democratic society 
have demonstrated their lack of regard for that society, and, particulary, 
the people in it.

They are simply not entitled to the protection it gurantees its citizens and 
those who are legal combatants. They choose to disregard the "rules" of war 
and of civilized society in general.

As a citizen of a democratic country, enjoying rights and freedoms similar 
to those enjoyed in the USA, I don't feel demeaned if terrorists are not 
accorded the same protection I enjoy. In my view, they lost those rights 
when they murdered innocent countrymen of mine. (So far, that hasn't 
happened but most of us realize that it could...)

We are a bit of a backwater here and we get used to being able to walk down 
the street without being shot at, or go to the movies without being blown to 
pieces. We are generally pretty laid back and very tolerant of the rights of 
others, particularly "underdogs"... :-) But I can assure you, if someone 
crashed a plane into downtown Auckland, that goodwill would evaporate very 
quickly. No-one would be shedding tears for the people who caused it, and 
there would be no problem with them not being subject to the same rules as 
the rest of us. There would be people who would insist that as the atrocity 
occurred on our soil, the perpetrators must be subject to our legal system 
and entitled to proper legal counsel and defence.

I think here would be relatives of people who were killed, who would simply 
say: "Fine, try them, then execute them."

Under our current legal system, it would not be possible to legally execute 
anyone. Another reason for making sure they are not given the standard 
rights of a citizen...

Please don't misinterpret what I'm saying; I am not eager to see ANYONE 
being executed, but there are some crimes for which the standard penalties 
simply don't cut it. (terrorism, abuse and murder of children, etc.) As a 
taxpayer, I see no reason why our taxes should be used to keep these people 
for decades with clean housing and three meals a day. Especially when there 
is no hope of ever rehabilitating them. It is cleaner and more efficient all 
around to simply execute them. (Note also, I am NOT advocating we torture 
them to death...it isn't about revenge; it is about cleaning up the mess and 
taking out the garbage.)

Usually, when the law is seen to be an ass, it either gets changed or people 
lose patience and take it into their own hands (I seem to recall the United 
States was founded by people taking such action against an unjust ruler...)

Summing up:

1. Terrorists are cowardly despicable scum who, by their actions have 
abnegated the right to be treated as civilized human beings. (People who 
have issues with democratic societies can employ means to resolve those 
issues that do not necessitate mass murder and maiming of civilian 
populations.)
2. Feeling that way does not demean me, my country, or the rights and 
priveleges being a citizen of my country bestows.

3. I have searched my conscience and simply cannot get soft on Terrorism.

Pete.

>they have already demonstrated, by action leading to the untimely death of 
>our citizens and destruction of our property, that they completely disagree 
>with our system and our society, and are dedicated to bringing it down by 
>violence?

Why should someone who is NOT a citizen have the same rights as other
non-citizens?

In times of peace, the state, just like the rest of us, has made
mistakes in accusing someone of a crime - and this has been more
common when there has been political pressure to find and punish the
guilty party.

Is this more likely or less likely to occur in times of war?    Does
it serve our purposes when people who might or might not support our
enemy see us abandon our principles in order to punish someone?

>> Rule of law doesn't say that anything is allowed, it says that the
>> government can't decide arbitrarily which people are allowed to defend
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>laws.

It is for the protection of all.    Civilized people don't punish the
innocent because they aren't a member of "civilized society".

opportunity, then they are candidates for my little needle.

comforted by virgins, knows his family will receive financial support, and

quite the same category. For him (given that his attempt failed) we should 
withold the needle and try reasoning. Certainly putting him away for long 
enough to reflect on his motives would not do society or him any harm.

admission) and proud of what they have done, would do it again in a

actions.

I watched a documentary last night following a squad of Taliban in one
of the northern provinces of Afghanistan. I was struck by a number of
points:

1. The apparent support for the Taliban from the locals.
2. The incompetence of the Taliban (it took them three attempts to
ambush a tank transporter and all they succeeded  in doing was wasting
one rpg (it missed) and detonating their roadside bomb after the event
because it failed to go off when the target approached).
3. The lies that the Taliban told their commander. They said they had
destroyed the tank and the 6-7 men escorting it. In fact they had
missed all targets. Further, they had claimed to have destroyed 6
tanks on the road in question but there was only evidence for the
destruction of one trailer or BMP (left over from the Russian
occupation).
4. The local police said that there were no Taliban in the area. This
despite the fact that the Taliban had been filmed shopping in the
local petrol station 2 days before the police visited the same shop.
5. That the Taliban had imposed local (sharia) law on the area
occupied and that they had become the de facto government because the
locals paid their taxes to the Taliban.

AND

6. One of the Taliban said (roughly): One thing that the US and
English should know is that when we have won the war in Afghanistan we
will come to Britain and Europe to impose Islam there.

I had my first heart attack at the age my father had his first. We've
both had stints, and I'm quickly approaching the age when he had his
final heart attack.

Genetics counts a lot.    Arthur Ashe was a world class athlete, but
he had a massive heart attack at the same age as the one that killed
his father.   Medicine (or maybe his active lifestyle) had increased
enough so that he survived - but not enough to screen his transfusions
for AIDS, and the transfusions ended up killing him.

```
Tony Harding wrote:
> On 01/21/10 17:45, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> 23?
Wow! That's a pretty amazing coincidence. My dad had his first heart attack 
at 38 and intermittent attacks from then until the final one in 1957. He was 
a very fit man, a PE instructor in the Army, and a professional boxer. The 
autopsy revealed heart muscle scarring from Rheumatic fever when he was a 
child in London. I still miss him so I guess that is as much a memorial as 
anyone can hope for :-)

I never think about when my life will be over or what percentage of the time 
it will last, has already expired. I have tried to live it to the fullest 
possible extent and am not unhappy with the results. Now, I feel more need 
for peace and quiet and am happy for it to enter a quieter, gentler, 
phase... :-) That's the main reason I have decided to stay in New Zealand 
and build a business from home. I'll never make a fortune at it (or even get 
anything like the income I have earned in the past) but I have realized I 
don't need to be fabulously wealthy, I just need to live like I am... :-)

Seriously, my needs are not great and as long as I can have the things I 
enjoy, I'll be very happy here. I love being home and there are things about 
this place which are beyond price.

My mother was told that I had 48 hours to live and there was nothing more 
they could do, when I was 14. (You can imagine how this affected her, having 
recently lost her husband... )

I'm, obviously, still here, but that experience changed my life. I resolved 
at the time that I would NOT die and would take every opportunity that life 
threw my way. For 50 years I have been living on "borrowed time" :-). I 
don't feel the universe owes me anything and when the time comes, like 
Robert Louis Stevenson, I shall "Lay me down with a will."

All of us could be dead within the next 10 minutes... it is only 
probabilities.

I wrote an article about this for a magazine column called "The Sharp Point" 
here a few months back. It was intended to help people dealing with loss and 
I had some very nice emails about it from people I never met.

The general gist was that the universe goes about its business without 
regard for "good" or "bad" outcomes. Events happen because that is the 
nature of things. The last sentence said something along the lines of "it 
isn't always snake eyes; sometimes you get a double six."

I don't accept the Fatalistic viewpoint although I agree that it is 
unarguable and if you believe it, you are right. (When there is only a 
single logic value ("The Will of God") it cannot be argued because it cannot 
be wrong. You accept it or you don't. I don't.)

Thre will come a time when I will no longer be posting here (sighs of relief 
all round... :-)) but until that time, I don't think there is anything to be 
gained by speculating when it might occur.

Pete.
```

...

>The general gist was that the universe goes about its business without 
>regard for "good" or "bad" outcomes. Events happen because that is the 
>nature of things. The last sentence said something along the lines of "it 
>isn't always snake eyes; sometimes you get a double six."

We will all die.   Even if they discovered a cure for aging and found
room for everybody, we will all die.   (at least if we live a life
remotely familiar to what we live now)

But seeing how much things change in, say 1000 years, I wonder how
long I would be fighting to live longer.    And 1000 years is nothing
compared to a million years, which is nothing compared to a billion
years, which is less than nothing compared to eternity.    I would
never sign a contract that required me to live forever, I'd want an
escape clause.

There are a fair amount of jokes involving playing golf in Heaven.
That is an interesting concept - what would your golf score be in
paradise?   Certainly it would not be worth playing if everybody
scored 18 in a round.   Perfection is boring.   So what is the
alternative worth having?    Life.   With its ups and downs, and
strivings and learning and worthy activities.

Yes, I think if you read Richard Dawking he comes across like that.

Everyone has their own stance on religion and spirituality and, as all of 
the data isn't in yet :-), it is hard to draw conclusions. I hold the views 
I do (confirmed Atheist) based on the research I did and my life experience. 
I have many friends who don't share my views and, over a bottle of wine or 
Jack Daniels, the conversations can become enthusiastic. :-) So far, I have 
lost no friends by discussing religion(or politics for that matter) probably 
because I know there can be more than one valid position on these topics, 
and I'm not out to Evangelize my position on it to anyone else.

I am always a little bit humbled, and touched, by friends who may be devout 
Christians and actually fear for my mortal soul.
(Some of them genuinely care and don't want to see me suffering the torments 
of Hell throughout Eternity.)
"What if you're wrong, Pete? What if you find yourself before God and have 
to account? "

They are very fair questions and I have given them some thought. (I have my 
defence well prepared, but if I lose, then I guess I'll cheerfully go to 
Hell... :-)) I remember as a child and, knowing my Dad could die any time, 
being concerned about what would happen to him. I asked him if he would go 
to Heaven or Hell and he smiled and said: "Son, I have friends in both 
places."

Thanks for reading the article, Tony, and for your kind words.

Glad you enjoyed it.

Pete.

omnibeneficent Being would dismiss a more-or-less well-lead life due to 
the absence of a stated 'I believe...'?'  After all, if such a Deity knew 
all, yea, even unto the numbering of the hairs of my head, might It not be

myself was true?

'Sorry, old boy... even though your work increased the quality of life for 
thousands, your treatment of others was, pretty much, courteous and the 
damage you caused was outweighed by the good that eventually resulted it 
says here that you didn't hold to the Infallibility of My Chosen 
Representative on earth.  Tell the crew with the horns and pitchfork-tails

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5aqlm59qku96r1nqd8v83utmokvse3vstf@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<7rf1t0Fsu3U1@mid.individual.net> <hk9uc202f33@news4.newsguy.com> <7sscciF3mrU1@mid.individual.net> <dcbjm5pujnmj4svvf4g7ipsbq1lct562s2@4ax.com> <hkeje7$bia$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```
On Thu, 4 Feb 2010 13:52:08 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>>Of course, what if they're wrong and Islam or Hinduism or some other
>>religion is right?  
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Dashwood's friends are positing seems to be a variant of Pascal's Wager.

Pascal's Wager makes some sort of sense if there are only two choices,
and one choice doesn't have a cost involved.

But with religions involved, there are lots and lots of choices, with
many religions and sub-religions ascribing a terrible cost for those
who choose wrong.

So if one wants to make that wager, it is worth while doing a
comparative study of all of the religions that promise unacceptable
punishment to the non-believers and pick either the most likely, or
the one with the worst such punishment.

>That's always struck me as 'what kind of omniscient, omnipotent and 
>omnibeneficent Being would dismiss a more-or-less well-lead life due to 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>myself was true?

Lots of people believe in an omnipotent, all-loving god who allows the
vast majority of His children to be tortured beyond all understanding
forever and ever without hope of parole - for the crime of being
fooled.

Actually, this is a variation of "My way is right, therefore your way
is wrong - and if you aren't sorry now that you don't think my way,
you will be sorry later".

But Churches and other organizations tend to build on their power by
codifying beliefs of infallibility.   Stories become Law, objection to
authority becomes treason, and servants become masters.

Also personal deities become unknowing, abstract entities that need
not make sense - so we have to accept whatever the Church says.

...

>>My son claims he never stopped believing in the Tooth Fairy, even
>>though he's paying out for his kids.    If he loses a tooth, he wants
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>sure to be on the losing side.

It's not about money, it's an amusing illustration of Pascal's wager.
```

On Dec 21 2009, 5:17 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:

You might like to know that the University of East Anglia have been
investigated for failing to provide information to a well known
climate skeptic within reasonable times. Unfortunately there is a time
limit under which UEA are let off the hook. However, the local police
force (Norfolk) are investigating and the failure to respond to the
Freedom Of Information request within a reasonable time period is to
be used to push for a change in the law to permit future non-
compliance to be punished.

Interestingly, whereas failing to respond to a FOI request may be a
civil offence, there is no word yet as to whether the more serious
criminal offence of fraud (by the hackers concerned) is to be
investigated.
